50ğ“€´While my husband and Mr. Henley were engaged in writing plays in
51ğ“€´Bournemouth they made a number of titles, hoping to use them in the
52ğ“€´future. Dramatic composition was not what my husband preferred, but
53ğ“€´the torrent of Mr. Henleyâ€™s enthusiasm swept him off his feet. However,
54ğ“€´after several plays had been finished, and his health seriously impaired
55ğ“€´by his endeavours to keep up with Mr. Henley, play writing was abandoned
56ğ“€´forever, and my husband returned to his legitimate vocation. Having
57ğ“€´added one of the titles, The Hanging Judge, to the list of projected
58ğ“€´plays, now thrown aside, and emboldened by my husbandâ€™s offer to give me
59ğ“€´any help needed, I concluded to try and write it myself.
60ğ“€´
61ğ“€´As I wanted a trial scene in the Old Bailey, I chose the period of 1700
62ğ“€´for my purpose; but being shamefully ignorant of my subject, and my
63ğ“€´husband confessing to little more knowledge than I possessed, a London
64ğ“€´bookseller was commissioned to send us everything he could procure
65ğ“€´bearing on Old Bailey trials. A great package came in response to our
66ğ“€´order, and very soon we were both absorbed, not so much in the trials
67ğ“€´as in following the brilliant career of a Mr. Garrow, who appeared as
68ğ“€´counsel in many of the cases. We sent for more books, and yet more,
69ğ“€´still intent on Mr. Garrow, whose subtle cross-examination of witnesses
70ğ“€´and masterly, if sometimes startling, methods of arriving at the truth
71ğ“€´seemed more thrilling to us than any novel.
72ğ“€´
73ğ“€´Occasionally other trials than those of the Old Bailey would be included
74ğ“€´in the package of books we received from London; among these my husband
75ğ“€´found and read with avidity:--
76ğ“€´
77ğ“€´     THE
78ğ“€´     TRIAL
79ğ“€´     OF
80ğ“€´     JAMES STEWART

82ğ“€´     FOR THE
83ğ“€´     Murder of COLIN CAMPBELL of Glenure, Efq;
84ğ“€´     Factor for His Majefty on the forfeited
85ğ“€´     Estate of Ardfhiel.
86ğ“€´
87ğ“€´My husband was always interested in this period of his countryâ€™s
88ğ“€´history, and had already the intention of writing a story that should
89ğ“€´turn on the Appin murder. The tale was to be of a boy, David Balfour,
90ğ“€´supposed to belong to my husbandâ€™s own family, who should travel in
91ğ“€´Scotland as though it were a foreign country, meeting with various
92ğ“€´adventures and misadventures by the way. From the trial of James Stewart
93ğ“€´my husband gleaned much valuable material for his novel, the most
94ğ“€´important being the character of Alan Breck. Aside from having described
95ğ“€´him as â€œsmallish in stature,â€ my husband seems to have taken Alan
96ğ“€´Breckâ€™s personal appearance, even to his clothing, from the book.
97ğ“€´
98ğ“€´A letter from James Stewart to Mr. John Macfarlane, introduced as
99ğ“€´evidence in the trial, says: â€œThere is one Alan Stewart, a distant
100ğ“€´friend of the late Ardshielâ€™s, who is in the French service, and came
101ğ“€´over in March last, as he said to some, in order to settle at home; to
102ğ“€´others, that he was to go soon back; and was, as I hear, the day that
103ğ“€´the murder was committed, seen not far from the place where it happened,
104ğ“€´and is not now to be seen; by which it is believed he was the actor. He
105ğ“€´is a desperate foolish fellow; and if he is guilty, came to the country
106ğ“€´for that very purpose. He is a tall, pock-pitted lad, very black hair,
107ğ“€´and wore a blue coat and metal buttons, an old red vest, and breeches of
108ğ“€´the same colour.â€ A second witness testified to having seen him wearing
109ğ“€´â€œa blue coat with silver buttons, a red waistcoat, black shag breeches,
110ğ“€´tartan hose, and a feathered hat, with a big coat, dun coloured,â€ a
111ğ“€´costume referred to by one of the counsel as â€œFrench cloathes which were
112ğ“€´remarkable.â€
113ğ“€´
114ğ“€´There are many incidents given in the trial that point to Alanâ€™s fiery
115ğ“€´spirit and Highland quickness to take offence. One witness â€œdeclared
116ğ“€´also That the said Alan Breck threatened that he would challenge
117ğ“€´Ballieveolan and his sons to fight because of his removing the
118ğ“€´declarant last year from Glenduror.â€ On another page: â€œDuncan Campbell,
119ğ“€´change-keeper at Annat, aged thirty-five years, married, witness cited,
120ğ“€´sworn, purged and examined ut supra, depones, That, in the month of
121ğ“€´April last, the deponent met with Alan Breck Stewart, with whom he was
122ğ“€´not acquainted, and John Stewart, in Auchnacoan, in the house of the
123ğ“€´walk miller of Auchofragan, and went on with them to the house: Alan
124ğ“€´Breck Stewart said, that he hated all the name of Campbell; and the
125ğ“€´deponent said, he had no reason for doing so: But Alan said, he had very
126ğ“€´good reason for it: that thereafter they left that house; and, after
127ğ“€´drinking a dram at another house, came to the deponentâ€™s house, where
128ğ“€´they went in, and drunk some drams, and Alan Breck renewed the former
129ğ“€´Conversation; and the deponent, making the same answer, Alan said, that,
130ğ“€´if the deponent had any respect for his friends, he would tell them,
131ğ“€´that if they offered to turn out the possessors of Ardshielâ€™s estate, he
132ğ“€´would make black cocks of them, before they entered into possession by
133ğ“€´which the deponent understood shooting them, it being a common phrase in
134ğ“€´the country.â€
135ğ“€´
136ğ“€´Some time after the publication of Kidnapped we stopped for a short

168ğ“€´MY DEAR CHARLES BAXTER:
169ğ“€´
170ğ“€´
171ğ“€´If you ever read this tale, you will likely ask yourself more questions
172ğ“€´than I should care to answer: as for instance how the Appin murder has
173ğ“€´come to fall in the year 1751, how the Torran rocks have crept so near
174ğ“€´to Earraid, or why the printed trial is silent as to all that touches
175ğ“€´David Balfour. These are nuts beyond my ability to crack. But if you
176ğ“€´tried me on the point of Alanâ€™s guilt or innocence, I think I could
177ğ“€´defend the reading of the text. To this day you will find the tradition
178ğ“€´of Appin clear in Alanâ€™s favour. If you inquire, you may even hear that
179ğ“€´the descendants of â€œthe other manâ€ who fired the shot are in the country
180ğ“€´to this day. But that other manâ€™s name, inquire as you please, you shall
181ğ“€´not hear; for the Highlander values a secret for itself and for the
182ğ“€´congenial exercise of keeping it. I might go on for long to justify one
183ğ“€´point and own another indefensible; it is more honest to confess at once
184ğ“€´how little I am touched by the desire of accuracy. This is no furniture
185ğ“€´for the scholarâ€™s library, but a book for the winter evening school-room
186ğ“€´when the tasks are over and the hour for bed draws near; and honest
187ğ“€´Alan, who was a grim old fire-eater in his day has in this new avatar
188ğ“€´no more desperate purpose than to steal some young gentlemanâ€™s attention
189ğ“€´from his Ovid, carry him awhile into the Highlands and the last century,
190ğ“€´and pack him to bed with some engaging images to mingle with his dreams.
191ğ“€´
192ğ“€´As for you, my dear Charles, I do not even ask you to like this tale.
193ğ“€´But perhaps when he is older, your son will; he may then be pleased to
194ğ“€´find his fatherâ€™s name on the fly-leaf; and in the meanwhile it pleases
195ğ“€´me to set it there, in memory of many days that were happy and some (now
196ğ“€´perhaps as pleasant to remember) that were sad. If it is strange for
197ğ“€´me to look back from a distance both in time and space on these bygone
198ğ“€´adventures of our youth, it must be stranger for you who tread the same
199ğ“€´streets--who may to-morrow open the door of the old Speculative,
200ğ“€´where we begin to rank with Scott and Robert Emmet and the beloved and
201ğ“€´inglorious Macbean--or may pass the corner of the close where that great
202ğ“€´society, the L. J. R., held its meetings and drank its beer, sitting in
203ğ“€´the seats of Burns and his companions. I think I see you, moving there
204ğ“€´by plain daylight, beholding with your natural eyes those places that
205ğ“€´have now become for your companion a part of the scenery of dreams. How,
206ğ“€´in the intervals of present business, the past must echo in your memory!
207ğ“€´Let it not echo often without some kind thoughts of your friend,
208ğ“€´
209ğ“€´R.L.S. SKERRYVORE, BOURNEMOUTH.

258ğ“€´I will begin the story of my adventures with a certain morning early in
259ğ“€´the month of June, the year of grace 1751, when I took the key for the
260ğ“€´last time out of the door of my fatherâ€™s house. The sun began to shine
261ğ“€´upon the summit of the hills as I went down the road; and by the time
262ğ“€´I had come as far as the manse, the blackbirds were whistling in the
263ğ“€´garden lilacs, and the mist that hung around the valley in the time of
264ğ“€´the dawn was beginning to arise and die away.

266ğ“€´Mr. Campbell, the minister of Essendean, was waiting for me by the
267ğ“€´garden gate, good man! He asked me if I had breakfasted; and hearing
268ğ“€´that I lacked for nothing, he took my hand in both of his and clapped it

370ğ“€´
371ğ“€´With that he got upon his feet, took off his hat, and prayed a little

384ğ“€´
385ğ“€´â€œDavie, Davie,â€ I thought, â€œwas ever seen such black ingratitude? Can

388ğ“€´
389ğ“€´And I sat down on the boulder the good man had just left, and opened the

393ğ“€´shilling piece; and the third, which was to help me so wonderfully both

395ğ“€´coarse yellow paper, written upon thus in red ink:

397ğ“€´
398ğ“€´â€œTO MAKE LILLY OF THE VALLEY WATER.--Take the flowers of lilly of the
399ğ“€´valley and distil them in sack, and drink a spooneful or two as there is
400ğ“€´occasion. It restores speech to those that have the dumb palsey. It is

2300ğ“€´At that period (so soon after the forty-five) there were many exiled

8335ğ“€´till I stepped after him, and gave him my hand, and thanked him by title
8336ğ“€´as the chief spring of my success, that he began to smile a bit, and was
8337ğ“€´at last prevailed upon to join our party.
8338ğ“€´
8339ğ“€´By that time we had the fire lighted, and a bottle of wine uncorked; a
8340ğ“€´good supper came out of the basket, to which Torrance and I and Alan
8341ğ“€´set ourselves down; while the lawyer and my uncle passed into the next
8342ğ“€´chamber to consult. They stayed there closeted about an hour; at the end
8343ğ“€´of which period they had come to a good understanding, and my uncle and
8344ğ“€´I set our hands to the agreement in a formal manner. By the terms
8345ğ“€´of this, my uncle bound himself to satisfy Rankeillor as to his
8346ğ“€´intromissions, and to pay me two clear thirds of the yearly income of
8347ğ“€´Shaws.
8348ğ“€´
8349ğ“€´So the beggar in the ballad had come home; and when I lay down that
8350ğ“€´night on the kitchen chests, I was a man of means and had a name in the
8351ğ“€´country. Alan and Torrance and Rankeillor slept and snored on their hard
8352ğ“€´beds; but for me who had lain out under heaven and upon dirt and stones,
8353ğ“€´so many days and nights, and often with an empty belly, and in fear
8354ğ“€´of death, this good change in my case unmanned me more than any of the
8355ğ“€´former evil ones; and I lay till dawn, looking at the fire on the roof
8356ğ“€´and planning the future.
8357ğ“€´
8358ğ“€´
8359ğ“€´
8360ğ“€´
8361ğ“€´CHAPTER XXX
8362ğ“€´
8363ğ“€´GOOD-BYE
8364ğ“€´
8365ğ“€´
8366ğ“€´So far as I was concerned myself, I had come to port; but I had still
8367ğ“€´Alan, to whom I was so much beholden, on my hands; and I felt besides a
8368ğ“€´heavy charge in the matter of the murder and James of the Glens. On both
8369ğ“€´these heads I unbosomed to Rankeillor the next morning, walking to and
8370ğ“€´fro about six of the clock before the house of Shaws, and with nothing
8371ğ“€´in view but the fields and woods that had been my ancestorsâ€™ and were
8372ğ“€´now mine. Even as I spoke on these grave subjects, my eye would take a
8373ğ“€´glad bit of a run over the prospect, and my heart jump with pride.
8374ğ“€´
8375ğ“€´About my clear duty to my friend, the lawyer had no doubt. I must help
8376ğ“€´him out of the county at whatever risk; but in the case of James, he was
8377ğ“€´of a different mind.
8378ğ“€´
8379ğ“€´â€œMr. Thomson,â€ says he, â€œis one thing, Mr. Thomsonâ€™s kinsman quite
8380ğ“€´another. I know little of the facts, but I gather that a great noble
8381ğ“€´(whom we will call, if you like, the D. of A.)[36] has some concern and
8382ğ“€´is even supposed to feel some animosity in the matter. The D. of A. is
8383ğ“€´doubtless an excellent nobleman; but, Mr. David, _timeo qui nocuere
8384ğ“€´deos_. If you interfere to balk his vengeance, you should remember
8385ğ“€´there is one way to shut your testimony out; and that is to put you in
8386ğ“€´the dock. There, you would be in the same pickle as Mr. Thomsonâ€™s
8387ğ“€´kinsman. You will object that you are innocent; well, but so is he. And
8388ğ“€´to be tried for your life before a Highland jury, on a Highland quarrel
8389ğ“€´and with a Highland Judge upon the bench, would be a brief transition
8390ğ“€´to the gallows.â€
8391ğ“€´
8392ğ“€´     [36] The Duke of Argyle.
8393ğ“€´
8394ğ“€´Now I had made all these reasonings before and found no very good reply
8395ğ“€´to them; so I put on all the simplicity I could. â€œIn that case, sir,â€
8396ğ“€´ said I, â€œI would just have to be hanged--would I not?â€
8397ğ“€´
8398ğ“€´â€œMy dear boy,â€ cries he, â€œgo in Godâ€™s name, and do what you think is
8399ğ“€´right. It is a poor thought that at my time of life I should be advising
8400ğ“€´you to choose the safe and shameful; and I take it back with an apology.
8401ğ“€´Go and do your duty; and be hanged, if you must, like a gentleman. There
8402ğ“€´are worse things in the world than to be hanged.â€
8403ğ“€´
8404ğ“€´â€œNot many, sir,â€ said I, smiling.
8405ğ“€´
8406ğ“€´â€œWhy, yes, sir,â€ he cried, â€œvery many. And it would be ten times better
8407ğ“€´for your uncle (to go no farther afield) if he were dangling decently
8408ğ“€´upon a gibbet.â€
8409ğ“€´
8410ğ“€´Thereupon he turned into the house (still in a great fervour of mind,
8411ğ“€´so that I saw I had pleased him heartily) and there he wrote me two
8412ğ“€´letters, making his comments on them as he wrote.
8413ğ“€´
8414ğ“€´â€œThis,â€ says he, â€œis to my bankers, the British Linen Company, placing a
8415ğ“€´credit to your name. Consult Mr. Thomson, he will know of ways; and
8416ğ“€´you, with this credit, can supply the means. I trust you will be a good
8417ğ“€´husband of your money; but in the affair of a friend like Mr. Thomson,
8418ğ“€´I would be even prodigal. Then for his kinsman, there is no better way
8419ğ“€´than that you should seek the Advocate, tell him your tale, and offer
8420ğ“€´testimony; whether he may take it or not, is quite another matter, and
8421ğ“€´will turn on the D. of A. Now, that you may reach the Lord Advocate well
8422ğ“€´recommended, I give you here a letter to a namesake of your own, the
8423ğ“€´learned Mr. Balfour of Pilrig, a man whom I esteem. It will look better
8424ğ“€´that you should be presented by one of your own name; and the laird of
8425ğ“€´Pilrig is much looked up to in the Faculty and stands well with Lord
8426ğ“€´Advocate Grant. I would not trouble him, if I were you, with any
8427ğ“€´particulars; and (do you know?) I think it would be needless to refer to
8428ğ“€´Mr. Thomson. Form yourself upon the laird, he is a good model; when you
8429ğ“€´deal with the Advocate, be discreet; and in all these matters, may the
8430ğ“€´Lord guide you, Mr. David!â€
8431ğ“€´
8432ğ“€´Thereupon he took his farewell, and set out with Torrance for the Ferry,
8433ğ“€´while Alan and I turned our faces for the city of Edinburgh. As we went
8434ğ“€´by the footpath and beside the gateposts and the unfinished lodge, we
8435ğ“€´kept looking back at the house of my fathers. It stood there, bare and
8436ğ“€´great and smokeless, like a place not lived in; only in one of the top
8437ğ“€´windows, there was the peak of a nightcap bobbing up and down and back
8438ğ“€´and forward, like the head of a rabbit from a burrow. I had little
8439ğ“€´welcome when I came, and less kindness while I stayed; but at least I
8440ğ“€´was watched as I went away.
8441ğ“€´
8442ğ“€´Alan and I went slowly forward upon our way, having little heart either
8443ğ“€´to walk or speak. The same thought was uppermost in both, that we were
8444ğ“€´near the time of our parting; and remembrance of all the bygone days
8445ğ“€´sate upon us sorely. We talked indeed of what should be done; and it
8446ğ“€´was resolved that Alan should keep to the county, biding now here, now
8447ğ“€´there, but coming once in the day to a particular place where I might be
8448ğ“€´able to communicate with him, either in my own person or by messenger.
8449ğ“€´In the meanwhile, I was to seek out a lawyer, who was an Appin Stewart,
8450ğ“€´and a man therefore to be wholly trusted; and it should be his part to
8451ğ“€´find a ship and to arrange for Alanâ€™s safe embarkation. No sooner was
8452ğ“€´this business done, than the words seemed to leave us; and though I
8453ğ“€´would seek to jest with Alan under the name of Mr. Thomson, and he with
8454ğ“€´me on my new clothes and my estate, you could feel very well that we
8455ğ“€´were nearer tears than laughter.
8456ğ“€´
8457ğ“€´We came the by-way over the hill of Corstorphine; and when we got
8458ğ“€´near to the place called Rest-and-be-Thankful, and looked down on
8459ğ“€´Corstorphine bogs and over to the city and the castle on the hill, we
8460ğ“€´both stopped, for we both knew without a word said that we had come to
8461ğ“€´where our ways parted. Here he repeated to me once again what had been
8462ğ“€´agreed upon between us: the address of the lawyer, the daily hour at
8463ğ“€´which Alan might be found, and the signals that were to be made by any
8464ğ“€´that came seeking him. Then I gave what money I had (a guinea or two of
8465ğ“€´Rankeillorâ€™s) so that he should not starve in the meanwhile; and then we
8466ğ“€´stood a space, and looked over at Edinburgh in silence.
8467ğ“€´
8468ğ“€´â€œWell, good-bye,â€ said Alan, and held out his left hand.
8469ğ“€´
8470ğ“€´â€œGood-bye,â€ said I, and gave the hand a little grasp, and went off down
8471ğ“€´hill.
8472ğ“€´
8473ğ“€´Neither one of us looked the other in the face, nor so long as he was in
8474ğ“€´my view did I take one back glance at the friend I was leaving. But as
8475ğ“€´I went on my way to the city, I felt so lost and lonesome, that I could
8476ğ“€´have found it in my heart to sit down by the dyke, and cry and weep like
8477ğ“€´any baby.
8478ğ“€´
8479ğ“€´It was coming near noon when I passed in by the West Kirk and the
8480ğ“€´Grassmarket into the streets of the capital. The huge height of the
8481ğ“€´buildings, running up to ten and fifteen storeys, the narrow arched
8482ğ“€´entries that continually vomited passengers, the wares of the merchants
8483ğ“€´in their windows, the hubbub and endless stir, the foul smells and the
8484ğ“€´fine clothes, and a hundred other particulars too small to mention,
8485ğ“€´struck me into a kind of stupor of surprise, so that I let the crowd
8486ğ“€´carry me to and fro; and yet all the time what I was thinking of was
8487ğ“€´Alan at Rest-and-be-Thankful; and all the time (although you would think
8488ğ“€´I would not choose but be delighted with these braws and novelties)
8489ğ“€´there was a cold gnawing in my inside like a remorse for something
8490ğ“€´wrong.
8491ğ“€´
8492ğ“€´The hand of Providence brought me in my drifting to the very doors of
8493ğ“€´the British Linen Companyâ€™s bank.
